<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_212468931">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/212468931" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5779732-kat" class="left imgcol" title="Kat"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1345551156p2/5779732.jpg" alt="Kat" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/212468931?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5779732-kat" class="user" title="Kat">Kat</a>

          added it




        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer212468931">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11391992681257151798">"You got to look at things with the eye in your heart, not with the eye in your head."-Lame Deer. <br /><br />I'm always on the hunt for great books by Native American authors. Unfortunately, most of what I've read thus far is from men. So when I saw this in a thrift store, I had to get it. I wasn't disappointed. The book is a quick read, but I drew it out for nearly a month because there is so much detail- I didn't want to miss anything. I'm particularly drawn to her chapter about the Sun Dance. Part of my</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11391992681257151798">"You got to look at things with the eye in your heart, not with the eye in your head."-Lame Deer. <br /><br />I'm always on the hunt for great books by Native American authors. Unfortunately, most of what I've read thus far is from men. So when I saw this in a thrift store, I had to get it. I wasn't disappointed. The book is a quick read, but I drew it out for nearly a month because there is so much detail- I didn't want to miss anything. I'm particularly drawn to her chapter about the Sun Dance. Part of my family is Blackfoot, and I'm curious to see if we have anything close to it.<br /><br />As a modern Native American who really has no ties to her tribes, let alone the BIA that's supposed to hold us "together," this book is an inspiration to continue fighting, even if I don't live on a rez. I've been accused of being an "apple"- red on the outside, white on the inside. And I'll admit, there's days I'm depressed as hell because I think they're right. Mary admits that members of the AIM have "calmed down," due to old age. Part of me wonders if my generation of Native Americans are ever going to have a movement, or is that window gone?<br /><br />We're all still struggling. Conditions on Pine Ridge are still harsh- there are still places without running water or electricity. The government is still trying to tell us what to do- even with our own people.<br /><br />We have to keep fighting, and this book solidifies that for me. We still have a long way to go for equality and atonement.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11391992681257151798">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_199087940">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/199087940" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/887926-brittany-kubes" class="left imgcol" title="Brittany Kubes"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1306809293p2/887926.jpg" alt="Brittany Kubes" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/199087940?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 18, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/887926-brittany-kubes" class="user" title="Brittany Kubes">Brittany Kubes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer199087940">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3354178117341016804">This book was a memoir by Mary Crow Dog, a half-blood Lakota woman who grew up in 1960-70 in a world where she was not white enough to be white, and not Indian enough to be Indian. The Lakota are a faction of the Sioux tribes that mostly occupy the Dakotas. After becoming an alcoholic at age 10, Mary joined the American Indian Movement (AIM), a group dedicated to advocating for Indian rights in the U.S. AIM is dedicated to achieving complete self-determination of Indians by Indians (no governanc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3354178117341016804">This book was a memoir by Mary Crow Dog, a half-blood Lakota woman who grew up in 1960-70 in a world where she was not white enough to be white, and not Indian enough to be Indian. The Lakota are a faction of the Sioux tribes that mostly occupy the Dakotas. After becoming an alcoholic at age 10, Mary joined the American Indian Movement (AIM), a group dedicated to advocating for Indian rights in the U.S. AIM is dedicated to achieving complete self-determination of Indians by Indians (no governance by the U.S. gov). <br /><br />I was generally perplexed while reading this book. Who was the intended audience? There was SO little context about Sioux and Lakota factions, the AIM movement, and her life, that I wondered if she was just writing this book for her Lakota friends and family. Also, the book states that it’s an “account of a woman’s triumphant struggle to survive in a hostile world,” when in reality it was mostly an account of her husband, Leonard Crow Dog, and his struggles to…I don’t know what? I wanted to be behind this book and the struggle of Indians to overcome white hostility, but it didn’t seem like that was the true focus of this book. <br /><br />I do think the legal issues for Indians are very interesting. Throughout the book, there is a lot of uprising against an alleged double standard where the killing of an Indian was usually treated as “a misdemeanor and went unpunished,” but if an Indian killed a white man he was condemned to death and was lucky to have it bargained down to a life sentence. I think this could be partially true only because of the PEOPLE involved in the legal system (i.e. racist prosecutors who decide charges, or racist jurors who find defendants guilty) and not due to actual statutory reasons.<br /><br />Perhaps due to the overwhelming angsty narrative and lack of details, I didn’t 100% trust the author and had a persistent feeling that she extrapolated a lot of facts. For instance, when relaying a story about a white man who killed an Indian, Mary claims he was originally charged with second-degree manslaughter (which the Indians protested, because it wasn’t first-degree murder) and then was eventually freed without doing any time at all. Well…1) she gave us no details of what happened in the alleged murder; 2) there are many legal reasons someone can be charged with second-degree manslaughter over murder (was there provocation by victim? act of self defense by killer?); and 3) it was unsaid whether he was ‘freed’ just because he was tried by a jury and found not guilty OR because the state dropped the charges.<br /><br />Additionally, Mary repeatedly refers to the time when the white hospital sterilized her sister in the ‘80s without her sister’s permission because the whites didn’t want more Indian babies…we do have a Constitution, don’t we? Equal protection, due process, something? HOWever, yes I realize it could be that I’m a naïve 21st century white girl, and just because we have laws, it doesn’t mean they were followed for the benefit of Indians 50 years ago. I do know (thanks to Constitutional Law class) that in the early 1900s, the U.S. had laws in some states that listed the insane, “feeble-minded,” and diseased as incapable of regulating their own reproductive abilities, which justified gov-forced sterilizations. I’ve also heard of coercion of minorities/disadvantaged to sign sterilization consent forms after such forms became mandatory. But I’m still torn about trusting Mary.<br /><br />Finally, in the middle of the book, Leonard Crow Dog (Mary’s husband) is convicted for the murder of another Indian. According to Mary, Leonard was sentenced to be hanged, then given 1 month to prepare himself for death (and the judge trusted him to come back to be executed solely on his word). Then, when he arrived for his execution 1 month later, Mary claims that Leonard’s lawyer says: “Crow Dog, you are a free man. I went to the Supreme Court for you and the Court ruled that the U.S. government has no jurisdiction over the reservation and that there is no law for punishing an Indian for killing another Indian.” ……………………….Is Mary referring to the case of Ex parte Crow Dog, in which the U.S. Supreme Court held that a federal court did not have jurisdiction over Indian Country (self-governing Native American communities, or reservations) and that there is thus no law for punishing an Indian for the murder of another Indian? Oh, OK, because this case happened in 1883. Also, the case would not have gone to the Supreme Court if it was already the law…the original court that convicted Leonard would not have convicted him. AND I think the 1883 case was modified by the Major Crimes Act of 1885, which made murder and manslaughter, among other crimes, subject to federal jurisdiction…this means that Leonard would have been subject to U.S. law, and rightfully “hanged.” Can someone resolve this for me? <br /><br />Overall: I might be focusing on the wrong things in this book, but I couldn’t help it. I wish there was more detail about Mary’s own life, because she seemed like such a strong person. I felt like weeping after reading the epilogue, where Mary briefly summed up her life after the heyday of her political activism in the ‘70s…she started with “The rest of the story is quickly told…” and ended with, “Life goes on.” <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3354178117341016804">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3501206?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/174487-lisa-lap" class="user" title="Lisa Lap">Lisa Lap</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/174487-lisa-lap?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/174487-lisa-lap?shelf=2013-books-read" class="actionLinkLite">2013-books-read</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3501206">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7015165248593794954">I have always found the stories of the American Indian people fascinating. I feel horror at many of the cruelties they endured as settlers took over this country and killed, captured, or confined the native people of this country. In this book, Mary Crow Dog gives an account of the events of her life during some of the last real battles the Indian people endured trying to keep their land and improve their living conditions. For the most part, these people only wanted to be left alone to continue</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7015165248593794954">I have always found the stories of the American Indian people fascinating. I feel horror at many of the cruelties they endured as settlers took over this country and killed, captured, or confined the native people of this country. In this book, Mary Crow Dog gives an account of the events of her life during some of the last real battles the Indian people endured trying to keep their land and improve their living conditions. For the most part, these people only wanted to be left alone to continue with their religion and ways of living without it being openly hated and sneered at by the "white" Americans. It is extremely sad to read how abusive and disrespectful of the people that lived here so long before we ever set a foot on the soil of this country. They lived in harmony with the earth, only using what they needed to survive and always honoring the spirit of the animals and Mother Nature that provided sustenance to them and their families. We were the plunderers that took more than what we needed and never stopped to appreciate the beauty of what we destroyed to make our towns and build a life on this earth. It saddens me to read how even today the Native Americans are still fighting to keep their simple lives in accordance with tradition while we continue to plunder and rapidly destroy so many of our natural resources. <br /><br />Until next time<br />~Lisa </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7015165248593794954">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/6758621-abby" class="user" title="Abby">Abby</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1224294215">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17571031673559794554">"I do not consider myself a radical or revolutionary. It is white people who put such labels on us. All we ever wanted was to be left alone, to live our lives as we see fit. To govern ourselves in reality and not just on paper. To have our rights respected. If that is revolutionary, then I sure fit that description. Actually, I have a great yearning to lead a normal, peaceful life--normal in the Sioux sense. I could have accepted our flimsy shack, our smelly outhouse, and our poverty--but only o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17571031673559794554">"I do not consider myself a radical or revolutionary. It is white people who put such labels on us. All we ever wanted was to be left alone, to live our lives as we see fit. To govern ourselves in reality and not just on paper. To have our rights respected. If that is revolutionary, then I sure fit that description. Actually, I have a great yearning to lead a normal, peaceful life--normal in the Sioux sense. I could have accepted our flimsy shack, our smelly outhouse, and our poverty--but only on my terms. Yes, I would have accepted poverty, dignified, uninterfered-with poverty, but not the drunken, degrading, and humiliating poverty we had to endure."<br /><br />Thoughtful.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17571031673559794554">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/565586997?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 30, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13940463-r-k" class="user" title="R.K.">R.K.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13940463-r-k?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer565586997">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17064789623821745337">I really enjoyed this book. Lakota Woman is easy to read and understand, and Mary Crow Dog has a bit of a poetic flow to her writing. I knew about the incident on Wounded Knee but this was the first I've read an account from a person who was there during almost the entire siege. It's one thing to hear about murder &amp; rape occurring on reservation land and knowing that it's happening because they "can get away with it" and then reading about the truth of that from someone who lives on a reserv</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17064789623821745337">I really enjoyed this book. Lakota Woman is easy to read and understand, and Mary Crow Dog has a bit of a poetic flow to her writing. I knew about the incident on Wounded Knee but this was the first I've read an account from a person who was there during almost the entire siege. It's one thing to hear about murder &amp; rape occurring on reservation land and knowing that it's happening because they "can get away with it" and then reading about the truth of that from someone who lives on a reservation.<br /><br />The way our government has treated the Americans here is shameful and many of these things are still happening, even today. I highly recommend this book for anyone interested in modern American culture and history because you won't find anything about the battle that happened at Wounded Knee in the 1970's or the racial torment of Leonard Crow Dog in ANY history books at school.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17064789623821745337">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer67536419">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13178937670816296204">It is difficult to review a book with repetitive writing and disorganized structure, but an important message. Mary Crow Dog's life illustrates many of the disconnects and difficulties of the American Indian, at least those of the South Dakota area. Her powerful message overcomes any writing issues and her raw delivery of it overcomes any apathy.<br /><br />The author carefully provides readers the histories and relationships of various tribes, as well as explanations of important religious ceremonies. Her</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13178937670816296204">It is difficult to review a book with repetitive writing and disorganized structure, but an important message. Mary Crow Dog's life illustrates many of the disconnects and difficulties of the American Indian, at least those of the South Dakota area. Her powerful message overcomes any writing issues and her raw delivery of it overcomes any apathy.<br /><br />The author carefully provides readers the histories and relationships of various tribes, as well as explanations of important religious ceremonies. Her courage, though raw and sometimes untempered, triumphs in light of the fact that she was a highly pregnant teenager at the 1972 siege of Wounded Knee, delivering a baby near the end. Her book is one of many to remind us of past tragedy in hope of a changed future. There cannot be too many of those.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/263414-erin" class="user" title="Erin">Erin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8475755">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9572952966573564439">This memoir brought up a lot of questions for me regarding the future of Native American cultures. Is there a way the U.S. government can make amends for all the horrible things it has done to Native American peoples? Do Native Americans only have two choices, to become acculturated to mainstream "white" society, or to live in poverty on reservations? I have to believe that the answer is "no".<br /><br />Recently, the Australian government officially apologized to Australian Aboriginal peoples for the unfor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9572952966573564439">This memoir brought up a lot of questions for me regarding the future of Native American cultures. Is there a way the U.S. government can make amends for all the horrible things it has done to Native American peoples? Do Native Americans only have two choices, to become acculturated to mainstream "white" society, or to live in poverty on reservations? I have to believe that the answer is "no".<br /><br />Recently, the Australian government officially apologized to Australian Aboriginal peoples for the unforgivable way they have been treated in the not to distant past. It will be interesting to see if this is a first step towards healing, and hopefully a better future for Australian Aborigines. Maybe the U.S. government needs to think about doing the same thing.</span>

Shortly before his death in August, 1950, when he was the &amp;quot;keeper of the sacred pipe,&amp;quot; he said, &amp;quot;It is my prayer that, through our sacred pipe, and through this book in which I shall explain what our pipe really is, peace may come to those peoples who can understand, and understanding which must be of the heart and not of the head alone. Then they will realize that we Indians know the One true God, and that we pray to Him continually.&amp;quot;\n\nBlack Elk was the only qualified priest of the older Oglala Sioux still living when The Sacred Pipe was written. This is his book: he gave it orally to Joseph Epes Brown during the latter\'s eight month\'s residence on the Pine Ridge Reservation in South Dakota, where Black Elk lived. Beginning with the story of White Buffalo Cow Woman\'s first visit to the Sioux to give them the sacred pip~, Black Elk describes and discusses the details and meanings of the seven rites, which were disclosed, one by one, to the Sioux through visions. He takes the reader through the sun dance, the purification rite, the &amp;quot;keeping of the soul,&amp;quot; and other rites, showing how the Sioux have come to terms with God and nature and their fellow men through a rare spirit of sacrifice and determination.\n\nThe wakan Mysteries of the Siouan peoples have been a subject of interest and study by explorers and scholars from the period of earliest contact between whites and Indians in North America, but Black Elk\'s account is without doubt the most highly developed on this religion and cosmography. The Sacred Pipe, published as volume thirty-six in the Civilization of the American Indian Series, will be greeted enthusiastically by students of comparative religion, ethnologists, historians, Women of Color Against Violence, the largest grassroots, multiracial feminist organization in the country, Andrea Smith (Cherokee) is an emerging leader in progressive political circles. In Conquest, Smith places Native American women at the center of her analysis of sexual violence, challenging both conventional definitions of the term and conventional responses to the problem.\n\nBeginning with the impact of the abuses inflicted on Native American children at state-sanctioned boarding schools from the 1880s to the 1980s, Smith adroitly expands our conception of violence to include environmental racism, population control and the widespread appropriation of Indian cultural practices by whites and other non-natives. Smith deftly connects these and other examples of historical and contemporary colonialism to the high rates of violence against Native American women—the most likely women in the United States to die of poverty-related illnesses, be victims of rape and suffer partner abuse.\n\nEssential reading for scholars and activists, Conquest is the powerful synthesis of Andrea Smith’s intellectual and political work to date. By focusing on the impact of sexual violence on Native American women, Smith articulates an agenda that is compelling to feminists, Native Americans, other people of color and all who are committed to Black Americans are fighting for civil rights, the counterculture is trying to subvert the Vietnam War, and women are fighting for their liberation. Indians were fighting, too, though it\'s a fight to&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText17786893691864127768\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;It\'s the mid-1960s, and everyone is fighting back. Black Americans are fighting for civil rights, the counterculture is trying to subvert the Vietnam War, and women are fighting for their liberation. Indians were fighting, too, though it\'s a fight too few have documented, and even fewer remember. At the time, newspapers and television broadcasts were filled with images of Indian activists staging dramatic events such as the seizure of Alcatraz in 1969, the storming of the Bureau of Indian Affairs building on the eve of Nixon\'s re-election in 1972, and the American Indian Movement (AIM)-supported seizure of Wounded Knee by the Oglala Sioux in 1973. Like a Hurricane puts these events into historical context and provides one of the first narrative accounts of that momentous period.Unlike most other books written about American Indians, this book does not seek to persuade readers that government policies were cruel and misguided. Nor is it told from the perspective of outsiders looking in. Written by two American Indians, Paul Chaat Smith and Robert Allen Warrior, Like a Hurricane is a gripping account of how for a brief, but brilliant season Indians strategized to change the course and tone of American Indian-U.S. government interaction. Unwaveringly honest, it analyzes not only the period\'s successes but also its failures.Smith and Warrior have gathered together the stories of both the leaders and foot soldiers of AIM, conservative tribal leaders, top White House aides, and the ordinary citizens caught up in the maelstrom of activity that would shape a new generation of political thought. Here are insider accounts of how local groups coalesced to form a national movement for change. Here, too, is a clear-eyed assessment of the period\'s key leaders: the fancy dance revolutionary Clyde Warrior, the enigmatic Hank Adams, and AIM leaders Dennis Banks and Russell Means. The result is a human story of drama, sacrifice, triumph, and tragedy that gives a ground-level view of events that forever changed the lives of Americans, particularly American Drawing from a wide range of sources - traditional narratives, Indian autobiographies, government transcripts, firsthand interviews, and more - Nabokov has assembled a remarkably rich and vivid collection, representing nothing less than an alternative history of North America. Beginning with the Indian\'s first encounters with the earliest explorers, traders, missionaries, settlers, and soldiers and continuing to the present, Native American Testimony presents an authentic, 4.22 avg rating &amp;mdash; 2,555 ratings&lt;\/span&gt;            &amp;mdash; published 1983\n          &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n    &lt;div class=\"addBookTipDescription\"&gt;\n      \n&lt;span id=\"freeTextContainer2221997117859016919\"&gt;An “indescribably touching, extraordinarily intelligent\" (Los Angeles Times Book Review) chronicle of a fatal gun-battle between FBI agents and American Indian Movement activists by renowned writer Peter Matthiessen (1927-2014), author of the Nationa&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText2221997117859016919\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;An “indescribably touching, extraordinarily intelligent&amp;quot; (Los Angeles Times Book Review) chronicle of a fatal gun-battle between FBI agents and American Indian Movement activists by renowned writer Peter Matthiessen (1927-2014), author of the National Book Award-winning The Snow Leopard and the new novel In Paradise On a hot June morning in 1975, a desperate shoot-out between FBI agents and Native Americans near Wounded Knee, South Dakota, left an Indian and two federal agents dead. Four members of the American Indian Movement were indicted on murder charges, and one, Leonard Peltier, was convicted and is now serving consecutive life sentences in a federal penitentiary. Behind this violent chain of events lie issues of great complexity and profound historical resonance, brilliantly explicated by Peter Matthiessen in this controversial book. Kept off the shelves for eight years because of one of the most protracted and bitterly fought legal cases in publishing history, In the Spirit of Crazy Horse reveals the Lakota tribe’s long struggle with the U.S. government, and makes clear why the traditional Indian concept of the earth is so important at a time when increasing populations are destroying the precious resources of our     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/34263.Mary_Crow_Dog">About Mary Crow Dog</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer1357154825576537781">Mary Crow Dog (who also wrote under the name <a title="Mary Brave Bird" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/365263.Mary_Brave_Bird">Mary Brave Bird</a>) was a Sicangu Lakota writer and activist.</span>

    </div>
